<p>The <b>United States Marine Corps</b> (<b>USMC</b>), also referred to as the <b>United States Marines</b>, is a branch of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Armed_Forces" title="United States Armed Forces">United States Armed Forces</a> responsible for conducting <a href="/wiki/Expeditionary_warfare" title="Expeditionary warfare">expeditionary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amphibious_operations" class="mw-redirect" title="Amphibious operations">amphibious operations</a><sup id="cite_ref-OMFTS_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OMFTS-13">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> with the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a> as well as the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">Army</a> and <a href="/wiki/U.S._Air_Force" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Air Force">Air Force</a>. The U.S. Marine Corps is one of the seven <a href="/wiki/Uniformed_services_of_the_United_States" title="Uniformed services of the United States">uniformed services of the United States</a>.
</p><p>The Marine Corps has been a component of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Navy" title="United States Department of the Navy">U.S. Department of the Navy</a> since 30 June 1834,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> working closely with <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy_operating_forces_organization" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Navy operating forces organization">naval forces</a>. The USMC operates <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Marine_Corps_installations" title="List of United States Marine Corps installations">installations</a> on land and aboard sea-going <a href="/wiki/Amphibious_warfare_ship" title="Amphibious warfare ship">amphibious warfare ships</a> around the world. Additionally, several of the Marines' tactical aviation squadrons, primarily Marine Fighter Attack squadrons, are also embedded in Navy <a href="/wiki/Carrier_air_wing" title="Carrier air wing">carrier air wings</a> and operate from the <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carriers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The history of the Marine Corps began when two <a href="/wiki/Battalion" title="Battalion">battalions</a> of <a href="/wiki/Continental_Marines" title="Continental Marines">Continental Marines</a> were formed on 10 November 1775 in <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a> as a service branch of <a href="/wiki/Infantry" title="Infantry">infantry</a> troops capable of fighting both at sea and on shore.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_War" title="Pacific War">Pacific theater</a> of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> the Corps took the lead in a massive campaign of amphibious warfare, advancing from island to island.<sup id="cite_ref-USMC-WW2-I-I-2_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USMC-WW2-I-I-2-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-USMC-WW2-IV-II-1_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USMC-WW2-IV-II-1-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-USMC-WW2-V-VI-1_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USMC-WW2-V-VI-1-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> As of 2017, the USMC has around 186,000 active duty members and some 38,500 personnel in <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps_Reserve" title="United States Marine Corps Reserve">reserve</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DoD_Budget_and_Manpower_Details_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DoD_Budget_and_Manpower_Details-3">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> It is the smallest U.S. military service within the DoD.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission">Mission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Marine_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Mission">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As outlined in <a href="/wiki/Title_10_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 10 of the United States Code">10 U.S.C.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.law.cornell.edu/uscode/text/10/5063">§&#160;5063</a> and as originally introduced under the <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Act_of_1947" title="National Security Act of 1947">National Security Act of 1947</a>, three primary areas of responsibility for the Marine Corps are:
</p>
<ul><li>Seizure or defense of advanced naval bases and other land operations to support <a href="/wiki/Naval_warfare" title="Naval warfare">naval campaigns</a>;</li>
<li>Development of tactics, technique, and equipment used by <a href="/wiki/Amphibious_warfare" title="Amphibious warfare">amphibious landing</a> forces in coordination with the Army and Air Force; and</li>
<li>Such other duties as the President or Department of Defense may direct.</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Marines_training.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Marines_training.jpg/220px-Marines_training.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Marines_training.jpg/330px-Marines_training.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Marines_training.jpg/440px-Marines_training.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="666" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Marines_training.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>U.S. Marines in training</div></div></div>
<p>This last clause derives from similar language in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">Congressional</a> acts <i>"For the Better Organization of the Marine Corps"</i> of 1834, and <i>"Establishing and Organizing a Marine Corps"</i> of 1798. In 1951, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">House of Representatives'</a> <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Armed_Services" title="United States House Committee on Armed Services">Armed Services Committee</a> called the clause "one of the most important statutory – and traditional – functions of the Marine Corps". It noted that the Corps has more often than not performed actions of a non-naval nature, including its famous actions in <a href="/wiki/First_Barbary_War" title="First Barbary War">Tripoli</a>, the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>, <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Chapultepec" title="Battle of Chapultepec">Chapultepec</a>, and numerous <a href="/wiki/Counter-insurgency" title="Counter-insurgency">counter-insurgency</a> and occupational duties (such as those in Central America), World War I, and the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>. While these actions are not accurately described as support of naval campaigns nor as amphibious warfare, their common thread is that they are of an expeditionary nature, using the mobility of the Navy to provide timely intervention in foreign affairs on behalf of American interests.<sup id="cite_ref-Estes_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Estes-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Marine_band.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Marine_band.jpg/220px-Marine_band.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Marine_band.jpg/330px-Marine_band.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Marine_band.jpg/440px-Marine_band.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Marine_band.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Marine Band, dubbed "The President's Own"</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Band" title="United States Marine Band">Marine Band</a>, dubbed the "President's Own" by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>, provides music for state functions at the White House.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Marines from Ceremonial Companies A &amp; B, quartered in <a href="/wiki/Marine_Barracks,_Washington,_D.C." title="Marine Barracks, Washington, D.C.">Marine Barracks, Washington, D.C.</a>, guard presidential retreats, including <a href="/wiki/Camp_David" title="Camp David">Camp David</a>, and the Marines of the Executive Flight Detachment of <a href="/wiki/HMX-1" title="HMX-1">HMX-1</a> provide helicopter transport to the President and Vice President, with the radio call signs "<a href="/wiki/Marine_One" title="Marine One">Marine One</a>" and "Marine Two", respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The Executive Flight Detachment also provides helicopter transport to <a href="/wiki/United_States_Cabinet" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Cabinet">Cabinet members</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Very_Important_Person" class="mw-redirect" title="Very Important Person">VIPs</a>. By authority of the 1946 Foreign Service Act, the <a href="/wiki/Marine_Security_Guard" title="Marine Security Guard">Marine Security Guards</a> of the Marine Embassy Security Command provide security for American <a href="/wiki/Diplomatic_mission" title="Diplomatic mission">embassies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Legation" title="Legation">legations</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Consul_(representative)" title="Consul (representative)">consulates</a> at more than 140 posts worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The relationship between the Department of State and the U.S. Marine Corps is nearly as old as the corps itself. For over 200 years, Marines have served at the request of various Secretaries of State. After World War II, an alert, disciplined force was needed to protect American embassies, consulates, and legations throughout the world. In 1947, a proposal was made that the Department of Defense furnish Marine Corps personnel for Foreign Service guard duty under the provisions of the Foreign Service Act of 1946. A formal Memorandum of Agreement was signed between the Department of State and the Secretary of the Navy on 15 December 1948, and 83 Marines were deployed to overseas missions. During the first year of the MSG program, 36 detachments were deployed worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_mission">Historical mission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Marine_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Historical mission">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Marine Corps was founded to serve as an infantry unit aboard naval vessels and was responsible for the security of the ship and its crew by conducting offensive and defensive combat during <a href="/wiki/Naval_boarding" title="Naval boarding">boarding actions</a> and defending the ship's officers from <a href="/wiki/Mutiny" title="Mutiny">mutiny</a>; to the latter end, their quarters on ship were often strategically positioned between the officers' quarters and the rest of the vessel. Continental Marines manned raiding parties, both at sea and ashore. America's first amphibious assault landing occurred early in the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">Revolutionary War</a> on 3 March 1776 as the Marines gained control of <a href="/wiki/Fort_Montagu" title="Fort Montagu">Fort Montagu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Old_Fort_of_Nassau" title="Old Fort of Nassau">Fort Nassau</a>, a <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British</a> ammunition depot and naval port in <a href="/wiki/New_Providence" title="New Providence">New Providence</a>, the Bahamas. The role of the Marine Corps has expanded significantly since then; as the importance of its original naval mission declined with changing naval warfare doctrine and the professionalization of the naval service, the Corps adapted by focusing on formerly secondary missions ashore. The Advanced Base Doctrine of the early 20th century codified their combat duties ashore, outlining the use of Marines in the seizure of bases and other duties on land to support naval campaigns.
</p><p>Throughout the late 19th and 20th centuries, Marine detachments served aboard Navy cruisers, battleships and aircraft carriers. Marine detachments served in their traditional duties as a ship's landing force, manning the ship's weapons and providing shipboard security. Marine detachments were augmented by members of the ship's company for landing parties, such as in the <a href="/wiki/First_Sumatran_Expedition" class="mw-redirect" title="First Sumatran Expedition">First Sumatran Expedition</a> of 1832, and continuing in the Caribbean and Mexican campaigns of the early 20th centuries. Marines would develop tactics and techniques of amphibious assault on defended coastlines in time for use in World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawliss_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawliss-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> During World War II, Marines continued to serve on capital ships. They often were assigned to man anti-aircraft batteries. When gun cruisers were retired by the 1960s, the remaining Marine detachments were only seen on battleships and carriers. Its original mission of providing shipboard security finally ended in the 1990s.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Capabilities">Capabilities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Marine_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Capabilities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Marine-31st.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Marine-31st.jpg/170px-Marine-31st.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="113" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Marine-31st.jpg/255px-Marine-31st.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Marine-31st.jpg/340px-Marine-31st.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="667" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Marine-31st.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>U.S. Marines from the <a href="/wiki/31st_Marine_Expeditionary_Unit" title="31st Marine Expeditionary Unit">31st Marine Expeditionary Unit</a> training</div></div></div>
<p>The Marine Corps fulfills a critical military role as an <a href="/wiki/Amphibious_warfare" title="Amphibious warfare">amphibious warfare</a> force. It is capable of <a href="/wiki/Asymmetric_warfare" title="Asymmetric warfare">asymmetric warfare</a> with <a href="/wiki/Conventional_warfare" title="Conventional warfare">conventional</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irregular_warfare" title="Irregular warfare">irregular</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_warfare" title="Hybrid warfare">hybrid forces</a>.
</p><p>While the Marine Corps does not employ any unique capabilities, as a force it can rapidly deploy a combined-arms task force to almost anywhere in the world within days. The basic structure for all deployed units is a <a href="/wiki/Marine_Air-Ground_Task_Force" title="Marine Air-Ground Task Force">Marine Air-Ground Task Force</a> (MAGTF) that integrates a <a href="/wiki/Ground_combat_element" title="Ground combat element">ground combat element</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Aviation_combat_element" title="Aviation combat element">aviation combat element</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Logistics_Combat_Element" class="mw-redirect" title="Logistics Combat Element">Logistics Combat Element</a> under a common <a href="/wiki/Command_element_(United_States_Marine_Corps)" title="Command element (United States Marine Corps)">command element</a>. While the creation of joint commands under the <a href="/wiki/Goldwater%E2%80%93Nichols_Act" title="Goldwater–Nichols Act">Goldwater–Nichols Act</a> has improved inter-service coordination between each branch, the Corps's ability to permanently maintain integrated multi-element task forces under a single command provides a smoother implementation of combined-arms warfare principles.<sup id="cite_ref-Warren_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Warren-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The close integration of disparate Marine units stems from an organizational culture centered on the infantry. Every other Marine capability exists to support the infantry. Unlike some Western militaries, the Corps remained conservative against theories proclaiming the ability of new weapons to win wars independently. For example, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps_Aviation" title="United States Marine Corps Aviation">Marine aviation</a> has always been focused on <a href="/wiki/Close_air_support" title="Close air support">close air support</a> and has remained largely uninfluenced by air power theories proclaiming that <a href="/wiki/Strategic_bombing" title="Strategic bombing">strategic bombing</a> can single-handedly win wars.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawliss_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawliss-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div id="mwe_player_0" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; style=&quot;width:608px&quot;&gt;&lt;video id=&quot;mwe_player_1&quot;
