<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AAV-australia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/AAV-australia.jpg/220px-AAV-australia.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/AAV-australia.jpg/330px-AAV-australia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/AAV-australia.jpg/440px-AAV-australia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1268" data-file-height="1012" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AAV-australia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Marine <a href="/wiki/Amphibious_Assault_Vehicle" class="mw-redirect" title="Amphibious Assault Vehicle">Amphibious Assault Vehicles</a> emerge from the surf onto the sand of Freshwater Beach, Australia</div></div></div>
<p>The Marine Corps utilizes a variety of direct-fire rockets and missiles to provide infantry with an offensive and defensive anti-armor capability. The <a href="/wiki/Shoulder-launched_Multipurpose_Assault_Weapon" class="mw-redirect" title="Shoulder-launched Multipurpose Assault Weapon">SMAW</a> and <a href="/wiki/AT4" title="AT4">AT4</a> are <a href="/wiki/Shoulder-launched_missile_weapon" class="mw-redirect" title="Shoulder-launched missile weapon">unguided rockets</a> that can destroy armor and fixed defenses (e.g., bunkers) at ranges up to 500&#160;meters. The smaller and lighter <a href="/wiki/M72_LAW" title="M72 LAW">M72 LAW</a> can destroy targets at ranges up to 200 meters.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/FGM-172_SRAW" title="FGM-172 SRAW">Predator SRAW</a>, <a href="/wiki/FGM-148_Javelin" title="FGM-148 Javelin">FGM-148 <i>Javelin</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/BGM-71_TOW" title="BGM-71 TOW">BGM-71 TOW</a> are <a href="/wiki/Anti-tank_guided_missile" title="Anti-tank guided missile">anti-tank guided missiles</a>. The Javelin can utilize top-attack profiles to avoid heavy frontal armor. The Predator is a short-range <a href="/wiki/Fire-and-forget" title="Fire-and-forget">fire-and-forget</a> weapon; the Javelin and TOW are heavier missiles effective past 2,000 meters that give infantry an offensive capability against armor.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The USMC is currently seeking to purchase commercial off-the-shelf bullet-trap or shoot-through rifle-grenades.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-145">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> These grenades will provide individual Marines additional firepower and will allow indirect fire against targets in defilade, behind walls and buildings or rooftops and elevated positions at ranges between 30 and 150 meters.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-145">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ground_vehicles">Ground vehicles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Marine_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Ground vehicles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_vehicles_of_the_United_States_Marine_Corps" title="List of vehicles of the United States Marine Corps">List of vehicles of the United States Marine Corps</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:M1_strategic_mobility.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/M1_strategic_mobility.jpg/220px-M1_strategic_mobility.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="117" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/M1_strategic_mobility.jpg/330px-M1_strategic_mobility.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/M1_strategic_mobility.jpg/440px-M1_strategic_mobility.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1917" data-file-height="1019" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:M1_strategic_mobility.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Marine <a href="/wiki/M1_Abrams" title="M1 Abrams">M1 Abrams</a> tank offloading from a <a href="/wiki/Landing_Craft_Air_Cushioned" class="mw-redirect" title="Landing Craft Air Cushioned">Landing Craft Air Cushioned</a> vehicle</div></div></div>
<p>The Corps operates the same <a href="/wiki/Humvee" title="Humvee">HMMWV</a> and <a href="/wiki/M1_Abrams" title="M1 Abrams">M1A1 Abrams</a> tank as does the Army. However, for its specific needs, the Corps uses a number of unique vehicles. The <a href="/wiki/LAV-25" title="LAV-25">LAV-25</a> is a dedicated wheeled armored personnel carrier, similar to the Army's <a href="/wiki/Stryker" title="Stryker">Stryker</a> vehicle, used to provide strategic mobility.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Amphibious capability is provided by the <a href="/wiki/Assault_Amphibious_Vehicle" title="Assault Amphibious Vehicle">AAV-7A1 Assault Amphibious Vehicle</a>, an armored tracked vehicle that doubles as an <a href="/wiki/Armored_personnel_carrier" class="mw-redirect" title="Armored personnel carrier">armored personnel carrier</a>, due to be replaced by the <a href="/wiki/Amphibious_Combat_Vehicle" title="Amphibious Combat Vehicle">Amphibious Combat Vehicle</a>, a faster vehicle with superior armor and weaponry. The threat of <a href="/wiki/Land_mine" title="Land mine">land mines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Improvised_explosive_device" title="Improvised explosive device">improvised explosive devices</a> in Iraq and Afghanistan has seen the Corps begin purchasing heavy armored vehicles that can better withstand the effects of these weapons as part of the <a href="/wiki/MRAP" title="MRAP">Mine Resistant Ambush Protected vehicle program</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> The Marine Corps has ordered 1,960 MRAP vehicles, hoping to use them to <a href="/wiki/HMMWV_replacement_process" class="mw-redirect" title="HMMWV replacement process">replace HMMWVs</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Medium_Tactical_Vehicle_Replacement" title="Medium Tactical Vehicle Replacement">Medium Tactical Vehicle Replacements</a> on patrols in Iraq.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Oshkosh_Logistic_Vehicle_System_Replacement_(LVSR)" title="Oshkosh Logistic Vehicle System Replacement (LVSR)">Logistics Vehicle System Replacement</a> began replacing the <a href="/wiki/Logistics_Vehicle_System" title="Logistics Vehicle System">Logistics Vehicle System</a> in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-LVSR_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LVSR-149">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Prior to 2005, the Marines operated exclusively tube artillery – the <a href="/wiki/M198_howitzer" title="M198 howitzer">M198 155&#160;mm howitzer</a>, being replaced by the <a href="/wiki/M777_howitzer" title="M777 howitzer">M777 155&#160;mm howitzer</a>. However, the Marine Corps has expanded its artillery composition to include the <a href="/wiki/HIMARS" class="mw-redirect" title="HIMARS">High Mobility Artillery Rocket System</a> (HIMARS), a truck-mounted rocket artillery system. Both are capable of firing guided munitions.<sup id="cite_ref-JCLewis_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JCLewis-150">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aircraft">Aircraft</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Marine_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Aircraft">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aircraft.osprey.678pix.jpg" class="image"><img alt="color photo of four parachutists jumping from the open ramp of an MV-22 Osprey in flight" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Aircraft.osprey.678pix.jpg/220px-Aircraft.osprey.678pix.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Aircraft.osprey.678pix.jpg/330px-Aircraft.osprey.678pix.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Aircraft.osprey.678pix.jpg/440px-Aircraft.osprey.678pix.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aircraft.osprey.678pix.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Marine parachutists jumping from an <a href="/wiki/Bell-Boeing_V-22_Osprey" class="mw-redirect" title="Bell-Boeing V-22 Osprey">MV-22 Osprey</a> at 10,000 feet</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps_Aviation" title="United States Marine Corps Aviation">United States Marine Corps Aviation</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_active_United_States_military_aircraft" title="List of active United States military aircraft">List of active United States military aircraft</a></div>
<p>The organic <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps_Aviation" title="United States Marine Corps Aviation">aviation</a> capability of the Marine Corps is essential to its amphibious mission. The Corps operates both <a href="/wiki/Helicopter" title="Helicopter">rotary-wing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fixed-wing_aircraft" title="Fixed-wing aircraft">fixed-wing aircraft</a> mainly to provide <a href="/wiki/Assault_Support" title="Assault Support">Assault Support</a> and <a href="/wiki/Close_air_support" title="Close air support">close air support</a> to its ground forces. However, other aircraft types are also used in a variety of support and special-purpose roles.
</p><p>The light transport and attack capabilities are provided by the <a href="/wiki/Bell_UH-1Y_Venom" title="Bell UH-1Y Venom">Bell UH-1Y Venom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bell_AH-1_SuperCobra" title="Bell AH-1 SuperCobra">Bell AH-1 SuperCobra</a>, which is <a href="/wiki/H-1_upgrade_program" title="H-1 upgrade program">being replaced</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Bell_AH-1Z_Viper" title="Bell AH-1Z Viper">Bell AH-1Z Viper</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> Medium-lift squadrons flying the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_Vertol_CH-46_Sea_Knight" title="Boeing Vertol CH-46 Sea Knight">CH-46E Sea Knight</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sikorsky_CH-53_Sea_Stallion" title="Sikorsky CH-53 Sea Stallion">CH-53D Sea Stallion</a> helicopters are converting to the <a href="/wiki/Bell-Boeing_V-22_Osprey" class="mw-redirect" title="Bell-Boeing V-22 Osprey">MV-22 Osprey</a> <a href="/wiki/Tiltrotor" title="Tiltrotor">tiltrotor</a> with superior range and speed. Heavy-lift squadrons are equipped with the <a href="/wiki/Sikorsky_CH-53E_Super_Stallion" title="Sikorsky CH-53E Super Stallion">CH-53E Super Stallion</a> helicopter, eventually to be replaced with the upgraded <a href="/wiki/Sikorsky_CH-53K" class="mw-redirect" title="Sikorsky CH-53K">CH-53K</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AH-1Z_lands_on_USS_Makin_Island_LHD-8.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/AH-1Z_lands_on_USS_Makin_Island_LHD-8.jpg/220px-AH-1Z_lands_on_USS_Makin_Island_LHD-8.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/AH-1Z_lands_on_USS_Makin_Island_LHD-8.jpg/330px-AH-1Z_lands_on_USS_Makin_Island_LHD-8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/AH-1Z_lands_on_USS_Makin_Island_LHD-8.jpg/440px-AH-1Z_lands_on_USS_Makin_Island_LHD-8.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1994" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AH-1Z_lands_on_USS_Makin_Island_LHD-8.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A U.S. Marine <a href="/wiki/Bell_AH-1Z_Viper" title="Bell AH-1Z Viper">AH-1Z Viper</a> lands on <a href="/wiki/USS_Makin_Island_(LHD-8)" title="USS Makin Island (LHD-8)">USS&#160;<i>Makin Island</i></a> in 2010</div></div></div>
<p>Marine attack squadrons fly the <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_AV-8B_Harrier_II" title="McDonnell Douglas AV-8B Harrier II">AV-8B Harrier II</a>; while the fighter/attack mission is handled by the single-seat and dual-seat versions of the <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_F/A-18_Hornet" title="McDonnell Douglas F/A-18 Hornet">F/A-18 Hornet</a> strike-fighter aircraft. The AV-8B is a <a href="/wiki/V/STOL" title="V/STOL">V/STOL</a> aircraft that can operate from <a href="/wiki/Amphibious_assault_ship" title="Amphibious assault ship">amphibious assault ships</a>, land air bases and short, expeditionary airfields, while the F/A-18 can only be flown from land or <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carriers</a>. Both are slated to be replaced by 340 of the <a href="/wiki/STOVL" title="STOVL">STOVL</a> B version of the <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Martin_F-35_Lightning_II" title="Lockheed Martin F-35 Lightning II">F-35 Lightning II</a><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> and 80 of the carrier <a href="/wiki/F-35C" class="mw-redirect" title="F-35C">F-35C</a> versions for deployment with Navy <a href="/wiki/Carrier_air_wing" title="Carrier air wing">carrier air wings</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_111003-N-ZZ999-002_An_F-35B_Lightning_II_makes_the_first_vertical_landing_on_a_flight_deck_at_sea_aboard_the_amphibious_assault_ship_USS_W.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/US_Navy_111003-N-ZZ999-002_An_F-35B_Lightning_II_makes_the_first_vertical_landing_on_a_flight_deck_at_sea_aboard_the_amphibious_assault_ship_USS_W.jpg/220px-US_Navy_111003-N-ZZ999-002_An_F-35B_Lightning_II_makes_the_first_vertical_landing_on_a_flight_deck_at_sea_aboard_the_amphibious_assault_ship_USS_W.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/US_Navy_111003-N-ZZ999-002_An_F-35B_Lightning_II_makes_the_first_vertical_landing_on_a_flight_deck_at_sea_aboard_the_amphibious_assault_ship_USS_W.jpg/330px-US_Navy_111003-N-ZZ999-002_An_F-35B_Lightning_II_makes_the_first_vertical_landing_on_a_flight_deck_at_sea_aboard_the_amphibious_assault_ship_USS_W.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/US_Navy_111003-N-ZZ999-002_An_F-35B_Lightning_II_makes_the_first_vertical_landing_on_a_flight_deck_at_sea_aboard_the_amphibious_assault_ship_USS_W.jpg/440px-US_Navy_111003-N-ZZ999-002_An_F-35B_Lightning_II_makes_the_first_vertical_landing_on_a_flight_deck_at_sea_aboard_the_amphibious_assault_ship_USS_W.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2100" data-file-height="1500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_111003-N-ZZ999-002_An_F-35B_Lightning_II_makes_the_first_vertical_landing_on_a_flight_deck_at_sea_aboard_the_amphibious_assault_ship_USS_W.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Marine Corps F-35B, the vertical-landing version of the <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Martin_F-35_Lightning_II" title="Lockheed Martin F-35 Lightning II">F-35 Lightning II</a> <a href="/wiki/Multirole_combat_aircraft" title="Multirole combat aircraft">multirole fighter</a> landing aboard <a href="/wiki/USS_Wasp_(LHD-1)" title="USS Wasp (LHD-1)">USS&#160;<i>Wasp</i></a></div></div></div>
<p>In addition, the Corps operates its own organic <a href="/wiki/Aerial_refueling" title="Aerial refueling">aerial refueling</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electronic_warfare" title="Electronic warfare">electronic warfare</a> (EW) assets in the form of the <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_C-130_Hercules" title="Lockheed C-130 Hercules">KC-130 Hercules</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northrop_Grumman_EA-6B_Prowler" title="Northrop Grumman EA-6B Prowler">EA-6B Prowler</a>, respectively, however it also receives a large amount of support from the U.S. Air Force. The Hercules doubles as a ground refueler and tactical-airlift transport aircraft. The Prowler is one of the only two active tactical electronic warfare aircraft left in the United States inventory, and has been labeled a "national asset"; it is used along with the Navy's <a href="/wiki/Boeing_EA-18G_Growler" title="Boeing EA-18G Growler">EA-18G Growlers</a> to assist in any American combat action since the retirement of the Air Force's tactical EW aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> The Marine Corps plans to retire the Prowlers by September 2016 and after that time EW capability will come from Marine Air-Ground Task Force Electronic Warfare, which is a strategy where every platform contributes and functions as a sensor, shooter and sharer.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Marines operate <a href="/wiki/Unmanned_aerial_vehicle" title="Unmanned aerial vehicle">unmanned aerial vehicles</a>: the <a href="/wiki/AAI_RQ-7_Shadow" title="AAI RQ-7 Shadow">RQ-7 Shadow</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boeing_ScanEagle" class="mw-redirect" title="Boeing ScanEagle">Scan Eagle</a> for tactical reconnaissance.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Marine Fighter Training Squadron 401 (<a href="/wiki/VMFT-401" title="VMFT-401">VMFT-401</a>), operates <a href="/wiki/Northrop_F-5" title="Northrop F-5">F-5E, F-5F and F-5N Tiger II</a> aircraft in support of air combat adversary (<a href="/wiki/Dissimilar_air_combat_training" title="Dissimilar air combat training">aggressor</a>) training. Marine Helicopter Squadron One (<a href="/wiki/HMX-1" title="HMX-1">HMX-1</a>) operates the <a href="/wiki/Sikorsky_SH-3_Sea_King" title="Sikorsky SH-3 Sea King">VH-3D Sea King</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sikorsky_UH-60_Black_Hawk" title="Sikorsky UH-60 Black Hawk">VH-60N Whitehawk</a> helicopters in the VIP transport role, most notably <a href="/wiki/Marine_One" title="Marine One">Marine One</a>, but are <a href="/wiki/VXX" title="VXX">due to be replaced</a>. A single Marine Corps C-130 Hercules aircraft, "Fat Albert", is used to support the U.S. Navy's flight demonstration team, the "<a href="/wiki/Blue_Angels" title="Blue Angels">Blue Angels</a>".
</p><p>In April 2016 it was reported that the vast majority of USMC aircraft were not capable of flight. Official statistics confirm that, of 276 F/A-18 Hornet strike fighters in the Marine Corps inventory, only about 30% are ready to fly; and only 42 of 147 heavy-lift CH-53E Super Stallion helicopters are airworthy. Average monthly flight time per pilot has dropped from an average of between 25 and 30 hours in 2006 to just over 4 hours in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_other_services">Relationship with other services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Marine_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Relationship with other services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In general, the Marine Corps shares many resources with the other branches of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Armed_Forces" title="United States Armed Forces">United States Armed Forces</a>. However, the Corps has consistently sought to maintain its own identity with regard to mission, funding, and assets, while utilizing support available from the larger branches. While the Marine Corps has far fewer <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Marine_Corps_installations" title="List of United States Marine Corps installations">installations</a> both in the U.S. and worldwide than the other branches, many <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Army_installations" class="mw-redirect" title="List of United States Army installations">Army posts</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Navy_installations" title="List of United States Navy installations">Naval stations</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Air_Force_installations" title="List of United States Air Force installations">Air Force bases</a> have a Marine presence. They also cross train with other countries.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States_Navy">United States Navy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Marine_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: United States Navy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="box-More_citations_needed plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=United_States_Marine_Corps&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br /><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22United+States+Marine+Corps%22">"United States Marine Corps"</a>&#160;–&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22United+States+Marine+Corps%22+-wikipedia">news</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22United+States+Marine+Corps%22+site:news.google.com/newspapers&amp;source=newspapers">newspapers</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22United+States+Marine+Corps%22+-wikipedia">books</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22United+States+Marine+Corps%22">scholar</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22United+States+Marine+Corps%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on">JSTOR</a></span></small></span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">November 2017</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USS_Bonhomme_Richard_(LHD-6)_welldeck.jpg" class="image"><img alt="color photo of swimming AAVs approaching the well deck of an amphibious assault ship" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/USS_Bonhomme_Richard_%28LHD-6%29_welldeck.jpg/220px-USS_Bonhomme_Richard_%28LHD-6%29_welldeck.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/USS_Bonhomme_Richard_%28LHD-6%29_welldeck.jpg/330px-USS_Bonhomme_Richard_%28LHD-6%29_welldeck.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/USS_Bonhomme_Richard_%28LHD-6%29_welldeck.jpg/440px-USS_Bonhomme_Richard_%28LHD-6%29_welldeck.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2100" data-file-height="1500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:USS_Bonhomme_Richard_(LHD-6)_welldeck.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Assault_Amphibious_Vehicle" title="Assault Amphibious Vehicle">Assault Amphibious Vehicles</a> approach the <a href="/wiki/Well_deck" title="Well deck">well deck</a> of <a href="/wiki/USS_Bonhomme_Richard_(LHD-6)" title="USS Bonhomme Richard (LHD-6)">USS&#160;<i>Bonhomme Richard</i></a></div></div></div>
<p>The Marine Corps's counterpart under the Department of the Navy is the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a>. As a result, the Navy and Marine Corps have a close relationship, more so than with other branches of the military. <a href="/wiki/Whitepaper" class="mw-redirect" title="Whitepaper">Whitepapers</a> and promotional literature have commonly used the phrase "Navy-Marine Corps Team",<sup id="cite_ref-Seapower21_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seapower21-163">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EnduringFreedomVid_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EnduringFreedomVid-164">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> or refer to "the Naval Service". Both the <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_Naval_Operations" title="Chief of Naval Operations">Chief of Naval Operations</a> (CNO) and <a href="/wiki/Commandant_of_the_Marine_Corps" title="Commandant of the Marine Corps">Commandant of the Marine Corps</a> report directly to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Navy" title="United States Secretary of the Navy">Secretary of the Navy</a>.
</p><p>Operationally, the Marine Corps provides the <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Marine_Force" title="Fleet Marine Force">Fleet Marine Forces</a> for service with the Navy's fleets, including the forward-deployed Marine Expeditionary Units embarked aboard Navy amphibious warships. The Corps also contributes some Marine Aviation fixed-wing fighter/attack assets (aircraft squadrons and related aircraft maintenance augmentation units) as part of the Carrier Air Wings deployed aboard aircraft carriers. The <a href="/wiki/Marine_Corps_Security_Force_Regiment" title="Marine Corps Security Force Regiment">Marine Corps Security Force Regiment</a> provides infantry-based security battalions and Fleet Anti-terrorism Security Team companies to guard and defend high-priority and overseas Navy bases. Security for the Presidential Retreat located aboard the <a href="/wiki/Camp_David" title="Camp David">Naval Support Activity Thurmont, viz., <i>Camp David</i></a> is provided by the Marine infantry battalion stationed as part of the garrison aboard Marine Barracks Washington.
</p><p>Cooperation between the two services includes the training and instruction of some future Marine Corps officers (most are trained and commissioned through Marine Corps OCS), all Marine Corps Naval Aviators (aircraft pilots) and Naval Flight Officers (airborne weapons and sensor system officers), and some Navy and Marine Corps enlisted personnel. The Corps receives a significant portion of its officers from the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Academy" title="United States Naval Academy">United States Naval Academy</a> (USNA) and <a href="/wiki/Naval_Reserve_Officers_Training_Corps" title="Naval Reserve Officers Training Corps">Naval Reserve Officers Training Corps</a> (NROTC). USNA and NROTC staff and faculty includes Marine Corps instructors. <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps_Aviation#Aviators_and_Flight_Officers" title="United States Marine Corps Aviation">Marine Corps aviators and flight officers</a> are trained in the <a href="/wiki/Naval_Air_Training_Command" title="Naval Air Training Command">Naval Air Training Command</a> (NATRACOM) and are designated, or <i>winged</i> as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Aviator" title="United States Naval Aviator">Naval Aviators</a> or <a href="/wiki/Naval_Flight_Officer" class="mw-redirect" title="Naval Flight Officer">Naval Flight Officers</a>. The Marine Corps provides flight instructors to the Naval Air Training Command as well as <a href="/wiki/Drill_instructor" title="Drill instructor">drill instructors</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Officer_Candidate_School_(United_States_Navy)" title="Officer Candidate School (United States Navy)">Navy's Officer Candidate School</a>. Many enlisted Marines, particularly those in the aviation maintenance specialties, are trained at Navy technical training centers. The Marine Corps also provides ground combat training support to various Navy field medical (Hospital Corpsmen), Naval Construction Force (Seabee), and Navy Expeditionary Warfare personnel, units, and commands.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:100419M3599F027.JPG" class="image"><img alt="A Marine (left) and a sailor (right) both dressed in combat gear, fire at a target on a desert weapons range" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/100419M3599F027.JPG/220px-100419M3599F027.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/100419M3599F027.JPG/330px-100419M3599F027.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/100419M3599F027.JPG/440px-100419M3599F027.JPG 2x" data-file-width="5616" data-file-height="3744" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:100419M3599F027.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Marine and sailor train with rifles in Iraq</div></div></div>
<p>Training alongside each other is viewed as critical, as the Navy provides transport, logistical, and combat support to put Marine units into the fight, such as <a href="/wiki/Maritime_prepositioning_ship" class="mw-redirect" title="Maritime prepositioning ship">maritime prepositioning ships</a> and <a href="/wiki/Naval_gunfire_support" title="Naval gunfire support">naval gunfire support</a>. Most Marine aviation assets ultimately derive from the Navy, with regard to acquisition, funding, and testing, and Navy <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carriers</a> typically deploy with a Marine squadron alongside Navy squadrons. Marines do not recruit or train noncombatants such as chaplains or medical/dental personnel; naval personnel fill these roles. Some of these sailors, particularly <a href="/wiki/Hospital_Corpsman" class="mw-redirect" title="Hospital Corpsman">Hospital Corpsmen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Religious_Programs_Specialist" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious Programs Specialist">Religious Programs Specialists</a>, generally wear Marine uniforms emblazoned with Navy insignia. Conversely, the Marine Corps is responsible for conducting land operations to support naval campaigns, including the seizure of naval bases. Both services operate a network security team in conjunction.
</p><p>Marines and sailors share many naval traditions, especially terminology and customs. Marine Corps <a href="/wiki/Medal_of_Honor" title="Medal of Honor">Medal of Honor</a> recipients wear the Navy variant of this and other awards;<sup id="cite_ref-Lawliss_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawliss-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> and with few exceptions, the awards and badges of the Navy and Marine Corps are identical. Much of testing for new Marine Corps aircraft is done at <a href="/wiki/NAS_Patuxent_River" class="mw-redirect" title="NAS Patuxent River">NAS Patuxent River</a>. The Navy's <a href="/wiki/Blue_Angels" title="Blue Angels">Blue Angels</a> flight demonstration team is staffed by both Navy and Marine officers and enlisted men, and includes a Marine <a href="/wiki/C-130_Hercules" class="mw-redirect" title="C-130 Hercules">C-130 Hercules</a> aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawliss_28-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawliss-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2007, the Marine Corps joined with the Navy and Coast Guard to adopt a new maritime strategy called <i><a href="/wiki/A_Cooperative_Strategy_for_21st_Century_Seapower" title="A Cooperative Strategy for 21st Century Seapower">A Cooperative Strategy for 21st Century Seapower</a></i> that raises the notion of prevention of war to the same <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_war" title="Philosophy of war">philosophical</a> level as the conduct of war.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> This new strategy charts a course for the Navy, Coast Guard and Marine Corps to work collectively with each other and international partners to prevent regional crises, man-made or natural, from occurring or reacting quickly should one occur to avoid negative impacts to the United States.
</p><p>The Marines have reduced the requirement for large amphibious ships from 42 to a bare minimum of 33 ships; the fleet currently stands at 29 ships and is likely to shrink in the future.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States_Army">United States Army</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Marine_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: United States Army">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">United States Army</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CSA-2006-01-12-095303_M249SAW.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/CSA-2006-01-12-095303_M249SAW.jpg/220px-CSA-2006-01-12-095303_M249SAW.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/CSA-2006-01-12-095303_M249SAW.jpg/330px-CSA-2006-01-12-095303_M249SAW.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/CSA-2006-01-12-095303_M249SAW.jpg/440px-CSA-2006-01-12-095303_M249SAW.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1989" data-file-height="1542" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CSA-2006-01-12-095303_M249SAW.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A soldier from the <a href="/wiki/1st_Infantry_Regiment_(United_States)" title="1st Infantry Regiment (United States)">1st Infantry Regiment</a> provides security for a joint Army-Marine patrol in <a href="/wiki/Rawa_(Iraq)" class="mw-redirect" title="Rawa (Iraq)">Rawa</a> in 2006. The <a href="/wiki/Shoulder_sleeve_insignia" class="mw-redirect" title="Shoulder sleeve insignia">shoulder sleeve insignia</a> has the logo of the <a href="/wiki/2nd_Marine_Division_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="2nd Marine Division (United States)">2nd Marine Division</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Marine Corps combat capabilities overlap with those of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">United States Army</a>, the latter having historically viewed the Corps as encroaching on the Army's capabilities and competing for funding, missions, and renown. The attitude dates back to the founding of the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Marines" title="Continental Marines">Continental Marines</a>, when General <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> refused to allow the initial Marine battalions to be drawn from among his <a href="/wiki/Continental_Army" title="Continental Army">Continental Army</a>. Most significantly, in the aftermath of World War II, Army efforts to restructure the American defense establishment included the dissolution of the Corps and the folding of its capabilities into the other services. Leading this movement were such prominent Army officers as General <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_Staff_of_the_United_States_Army" title="Chief of Staff of the United States Army">Army Chief of Staff</a> <a href="/wiki/George_C._Marshall" class="mw-redirect" title="George C. Marshall">George C. Marshall</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Krulak_69-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krulak-69">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Nonetheless- during the Korean War, the Marines received the admiration of such Army officers as General Douglas MacArthur, and Major General Frank E. Lowe. <a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="http://www.usmcpress.com/heritage/usmc_quotations.htm">[2]</a> More recently, with most of the 2000s spent in operations in Afghanistan and Iraq, Secretary of Defense <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gates" title="Robert Gates">Robert Gates</a> voiced concerns that the Marines are becoming a "second Army".<sup id="cite_ref-2ndarmy_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2ndarmy-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Doctrinally, the Marine Corps's focus is on being expeditionary and independent, and places emphasis on amphibious mobility and <a href="/wiki/Combined_arms" title="Combined arms">combined arms</a>; these make it a much lighter force than many units of the Army. However, the Army maintains much larger and diverse combat arms (infantry, armor, artillery, special operations), ground transport, and logistics, while the Marines have a more diverse aviation arm (which constitutes a larger percentage of forces), and is usually organic to the <a href="/wiki/Marine_Air-Ground_Task_Force" title="Marine Air-Ground Task Force">MAGTF</a>. Marines operate as expeditionary units and are completely <a href="/wiki/Amphibious_warfare" title="Amphibious warfare">amphibious</a>. The Marine Corps focus is on standardized infantry units with the other arms in support roles, as the "Every Marine a rifleman" creed shows. This commitment to standardized units can be seen in the short-lived experiment of the <a href="/wiki/Marine_Raiders" title="Marine Raiders">Marine Raiders</a>. Widely known as the first American special operations unit, created during World War II (February 1941<sup id="cite_ref-WWII_Raider_date_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WWII_Raider_date-167">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>), was seen as controversial, due to the thought of ‘an elite unit, within an elite unit’ was not in the Marine Corps interest.<sup id="cite_ref-an_elite,_within_an_elite’_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-an_elite,_within_an_elite’-168">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> While the U.S. Army's <a href="/wiki/75th_Ranger_Regiment_(United_States)" title="75th Ranger Regiment (United States)">75th Ranger Regiment</a>, also created in World War II (December 1941), enjoys high prestige to this day due to its continuous service. The Marine Corps, by the insistence of then Secretary of Defense <a href="/wiki/Donald_Rumsfeld" title="Donald Rumsfeld">Donald Rumsfeld</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Donald_Rumsfeld_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Donald_Rumsfeld-169">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> were to create the present day successors to the Marine Raiders and join them to <a href="/wiki/United_States_Special_Operations_Command" title="United States Special Operations Command">Special Operations Command</a> starting with the establishment of <a href="/wiki/MCSOCOM_Detachment_One" title="MCSOCOM Detachment One">MCSOCOM Detachment One</a> in 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-Priddy_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Priddy-105">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Marines often leverage the Army's acquisition of ground equipment (as well as benefiting from Army research and development resources), training resources, and other support concepts. The majority of <a href="/wiki/List_of_vehicles_of_the_United_States_Marine_Corps" title="List of vehicles of the United States Marine Corps">vehicles</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_weapons_of_the_United_States_Marine_Corps" title="List of weapons of the United States Marine Corps">weapons</a> are shared with, modified, or inherited from Army programs.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Reliable source needed for the whole sentence (June 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Culturally, Marines and soldiers share most of the common U.S. military slang and terminology, but the Corps utilizes a large number of <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms" title="Glossary of nautical terms">naval terms</a> and traditions incompatible with army lifestyle, as well as their own <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Marine_Corps_acronyms_and_expressions" title="List of United States Marine Corps acronyms and expressions">unique vernacular</a>. Many Marines regard their <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_the_United_States_Marine_Corps" title="Culture of the United States Marine Corps">culture</a> to have a deep warrior tradition, with the ethos that "Every Marine a rifleman" and emphasis on cross-training and combat readiness despite actual military occupation, be it infantry or otherwise. Doctrinally, Marines tend to decentralize and push leadership to lower ranks, while fostering initiative to a greater degree.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States_Air_Force">United States Air Force</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Marine_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: United States Air Force">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">United States Air Force</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:C-5_CH-46.jpg" class="image"><img alt="color photo of Marines pushing carted equipment from the open bay of a large cargo jet" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/C-5_CH-46.jpg/170px-C-5_CH-46.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/C-5_CH-46.jpg/255px-C-5_CH-46.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/C-5_CH-46.jpg/340px-C-5_CH-46.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="2250" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:C-5_CH-46.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Marines unload <a href="/wiki/Boeing_Vertol_CH-46_Sea_Knight" title="Boeing Vertol CH-46 Sea Knight">CH-46 Sea Knight</a> helicopters from an Air Force <a href="/wiki/C-5_Galaxy" class="mw-redirect" title="C-5 Galaxy">C-5 Galaxy</a></div></div></div>
<p>While some of <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps_Aviation" title="United States Marine Corps Aviation">Marine Corps Aviation</a> assets ultimately derive from the Navy, a large amount of support is drawn from the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">United States Air Force</a>. The Marine Corps makes extensive use of the USAF <a href="/wiki/Air_Mobility_Command" title="Air Mobility Command">Air Mobility Command</a> to <a href="/wiki/Airlift" title="Airlift">airlift</a> Marines and equipment, along with utilizing close air support from the Air Force. The Air Force may also attach <a href="/wiki/TACP" class="mw-redirect" title="TACP">TACP</a> units to conventional marine ground forces to provide coordination for close air support.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Air Force traditionally provides the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force#Aerospace_Expeditionary_Task_Force" title="United States Air Force">Joint Force Air Component Commander</a> who controls "sorties for air defense, and long range interdiction and reconnaissance" while the MAGTF commander retains control of the Marines' organic aviation assets, however Marine Aviation missions not directly in the support of the MAGTF will be typically controlled by the JFACC.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States_Coast_Guard">United States Coast Guard</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Marine_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: United States Coast Guard">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_Coast_Guard" title="United States Coast Guard">United States Coast Guard</a></div>
<p>The Marine Corps shares a sphere of operation with units of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Coast_Guard" title="United States Coast Guard">United States Coast Guard</a>, including operation of the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Maritime_Training_Center" title="Joint Maritime Training Center">Joint Maritime Training Center</a> (JMTC) (previously known as the Special Missions Training Center (SMTC)), a joint Coast Guard, Navy, and Marine Corps training facility located on the <a href="/wiki/Marine_Corps_Base_Camp_Lejeune" title="Marine Corps Base Camp Lejeune">Marine Corps Base Camp Lejeune</a> in <a href="/wiki/Camp_Lejeune,_North_Carolina" class="mw-redirect" title="Camp Lejeune, North Carolina">Camp Lejeune, North Carolina</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Budget">Budget</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Marine_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Budget">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

</p>

<li id="cite_note-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-20">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Coast_Guard" title="United States Coast Guard">United States Coast Guard</a> is smaller, about one-fifth the size of the U.S. Marine Corps, but it is part of <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Homeland_Security" title="United States Department of Homeland Security">United States Department of Homeland Security</a> and does not normally operate under the DoD except during times of war or as directed by the U.S. president. The USMC is the largest marine corps force in the world and is larger than the armed forces of many significant powers; for example, the USMC is larger than the active duty <a href="/wiki/Israel_Defense_Forces" title="Israel Defense Forces">Israel Defense Forces</a>.</span>
</li>
